Published by Elsevier B.V.</prism:copyright><prism:publisher>The Author(s). Published by Elsevier B.V.</prism:publisher><dc:creator>Sufyan, Ali</dc:creator><dc:creator>Sajjad, Muhammad</dc:creator><dc:creator>Andreas Larsson, J.</dc:creator><dc:description>
                  We present first-principles insights into the electrical and electrochemical properties of Cu2N, a newly synthesized two-dimensional material that features a planar, checkerboard lattice structure [Hu et al., Nano Lett. 2023, 23 (12), 5610–5616]. We evaluate the suitability of monolayer Cu2N as an anode material for Li and Na-ion batteries by examining its storage capacity, diffusion barrier, open-circuit voltage (OCV), volume expansion, and the impact of defects on its electrochemical performance. The monolayer Cu2N demonstrates a storage capacity of 379.88 mAh.g−1 for both Li and Na, comparable to that of commercial graphite for Li (372 mAh.g−1) and significantly higher for Na (less than 35 mAh.g−1). The migration barriers for Li and Na are found to be 0.1 eV and 0.01 eV, respectively, substantially lower than those theoretically reported for commercial anodes TiO2 (0.4–1.0 eV) and graphite (∼0.4 eV), which imply that monolayer Cu2N demonstrates excellent charge/discharge capabilities. Moreover, the volume growth of monolayer Cu2N is 4.14 % with maximal Li adsorption, which is 2.4 times less than graphite. The analysis of vacancy defects reveals a significant enhancement in the binding energies of Li and Na atoms, accompanied by minimal changes in diffusion barriers. Since monolayer Cu2N has already been successfully synthesized, these findings would pave the way for large-scale experimental fabrication of monolayer Cu2N as a battery anode.

            <ce:abstract class="graphical" xml:lang="en" id="ab005" view="all">
               <ce:section-title id="st005">Graphical abstract</ce:section-title>
               <ce:abstract-sec id="as005" view="all">
                  <ce:simple-para id="sp0005" view="all">
                     <ce:display>
                        <ce:figure id="f0045">
                           <ce:link locator="ga1" id="lk00045" xlink:role="http://data.elsevier.com/vocabulary/ElsevierContentTypes/23.4" xlink:href="pii:S0169433224001879/ga1"/>
                        </ce:figure>
                     </ce:display>
                  </ce:simple-para>
               </ce:abstract-sec>
            </ce:abstract>
            
            <ce:abstract class="author" id="ab015" view="all"><ce:abstract-sec id="as015" view="all">
                  <ce:simple-para id="sp0015" view="all">We present first-principles insights into the electrical and electrochemical properties of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, a newly synthesized two-dimensional material that features a planar, checkerboard lattice structure [Hu et al., Nano Lett. 2023, 23 (12), 5610–5616]. We evaluate the suitability of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N as an anode material for Li and Na-ion batteries by examining its storage capacity, diffusion barrier, open-circuit voltage (OCV), volume expansion, and the impact of defects on its electrochemical performance. The monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N demonstrates a storage capacity of 379.88 mAh.g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> for both Li and Na, comparable to that of commercial graphite for Li (372 mAh.g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>) and significantly higher for Na (less than 35 mAh.g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>). The migration barriers for Li and Na are found to be 0.1 eV and 0.01 eV, respectively, substantially lower than those theoretically reported for commercial anodes TiO<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> (0.4–1.0 eV) and graphite (∼0.4 eV), which imply that monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N demonstrates excellent charge/discharge capabilities. Moreover, the volume growth of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N is 4.14 % with maximal Li adsorption, which is 2.4 times less than graphite. The analysis of vacancy defects reveals a significant enhancement in the binding energies of Li and Na atoms, accompanied by minimal changes in diffusion barriers. Since monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N has already been successfully synthesized, these findings would pave the way for large-scale experimental fabrication of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N as a battery anode.</ce:simple-para>
               </ce:abstract-sec></ce:abstract>

            <ce:sections>
               <ce:section id="s0005" view="all">
                  <ce:label>1</ce:label>
                  <ce:section-title id="st030">Introduction</ce:section-title>
                  <ce:para id="p0030" view="all">In recent years, renewable energy sources have gained significant prominence as a sustainable solution to the world's growing energy demands and the need to mitigate climate change. Solar and wind energy, in particular, have witnessed rapid advancements and widespread adoption. However, the intermittent nature of these energy sources necessitates efficient and reliable energy storage solutions, making batteries a crucial component of the renewable energy landscape <ce:cross-ref refid="b0005" id="c0025">[1]</ce:cross-ref>. Besides, the rapid growth of portable electronic devices and electric vehicles has spurred intense research efforts to enhance energy storage technologies, including lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Central to these efforts is the exploration of novel anode materials that can offer higher energy densities, faster charge/discharge rates, and improved cycling stability. Graphite, though a conventional anode material in LIBs for decades, offers a limited theoretical specific capacity of 372 mAh.g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>, which has fostered the search for alternatives<ce:cross-refs refid="b0010 b0015" id="r0005">[2,3]</ce:cross-refs>. In this context, the emergence of two-dimensional (2D) materials has positioned them as potential contenders for anode applications in both LIBs and SIBs<ce:cross-refs refid="b0020 b0025 b0030 b0035 b0040 b0045 b0050 b0055 b0060 b0065 b0070 b0075 b0080 b0085" id="r0010">[4–17]</ce:cross-refs>. Their remarkable properties, characterized by high surface area, exceptional electronic conductivity, and distinctive structural characteristics, have fueled extensive interest in their potential as anode materials<ce:cross-ref refid="b0090" id="c0030">[18]</ce:cross-ref>.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0035" view="all">Graphene, acclaimed for its extraordinary electrical conductivity and mechanical robustness<ce:cross-ref refid="b0095" id="c0035">[19]</ce:cross-ref>, presents a compelling choice for anodes, where rapid electron transport is essential for high-rate charge/discharge processes. Additionally, graphene-based anodes have demonstrated superior specific capacities and cycling stability in comparison to graphite, attributed to their capacity to accommodate a greater quantity of Li/Na-ions<ce:cross-refs refid="b0055 b0100 b0105" id="r0015">[11,20,21]</ce:cross-refs>. In parallel, transition metal oxides (TMOs)<ce:cross-refs refid="b0025 b0110" id="r0020">[5,22]</ce:cross-refs>, transition metal carbides (TMCs) and nitrides (TMNs), also known as (MXenes)<ce:cross-refs refid="b0030 b0035 b0115 b0120" id="r0025">[6,7,23,24]</ce:cross-refs>, hexagonal transition metal borides (MBenes)<ce:cross-refs refid="b0040 b0045" id="r0030">[8,9]</ce:cross-refs>, and transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs)<ce:cross-refs refid="b0050 b0125" id="r0035">[10,25]</ce:cross-refs>, have been widely studied as electrode materials for Li and Na-ion batteries. Additionally, boron-based 2D materials have also emerged as a promising class of anode material for metal-ion batteries<ce:cross-refs refid="b0085 b0130 b0135 b0140 b0145" id="r0040">[17,26–29]</ce:cross-refs>. These 2D materials offer intriguing possibilities for Li/Na anodes due to their layered structures accompanied by substantial specific capacities, metallic conductivity (or narrow bandgap semiconducting characteristics), and good cycling stability. Among all those possible candidates, MXenes have received the most attention as promising anode materials<ce:cross-refs refid="b0150 b0155" id="r0045">[30,31]</ce:cross-refs>.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0040" view="all">MXenes, which were first synthesized by Naguib et al.<ce:cross-ref refid="b0160" id="c0040">[32]</ce:cross-ref> in 2011, are characterized by the chemical formula M<ce:inf loc="post">n+1</ce:inf>X<ce:inf loc="post">n</ce:inf>, where M is an early transition metal, X is carbon and/or nitrogen, and n is the thickness (n = 1, 2, 3). To date, over a hundred MXene variants have been proposed theoretically, of which approximately 30 have been experimentally synthesized<ce:cross-ref refid="b0115" id="c0045">[23]</ce:cross-ref>. Recent theoretical studies demonstrate that MXenes (such as Ti<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>C<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>, Ti<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>C, Nb<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>C, V<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>C, etc.) are excellent anodes for Li and Na-ion batteries because they have low diffusion barriers and a comparatively high storage capacity<ce:cross-refs refid="b0035 b0165" id="r0050">[7,33]</ce:cross-refs>. However, the structure of MXenes is not stable in oxygen and water environments, and their basal faces are frequently terminated with F, O, or OH surface groups<ce:cross-ref refid="b0170" id="c0050">[34]</ce:cross-ref>. Such terminations significantly reduce their storage capacity while simultaneously increasing the energy barrier for cation migration<ce:cross-refs refid="b0150 b0155" id="r0055">[30,31]</ce:cross-refs>. Therefore, it is important for future Li and Na battery advancements to explore new types of 2D TMCs/TMNs electrode materials that offer both high capacity and stability.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0045" view="all">There has been some theoretical work on the structural prediction of new type of 2D TMCs/TMNs and their application to LIBs/SIBs<ce:cross-refs refid="b0175 b0180 b0185" id="r0060">[35–37]</ce:cross-refs>, but there is still a large gap between theoretical proposals and real-world implementation. Bridging this gap requires the synthesis of TMCs/TMNs with new and more stable structural configurations and a thorough exploration of their potential as anode materials for LIBs/SIBs. In this context, Hu and co-workers<ce:cross-ref refid="b0190" id="c0055">[38]</ce:cross-ref> have recently synthesized a new 2D TMN, Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, with a checkerboard lattice configuration via molecular beam epitaxy on Cu(1<ce:hsp sp="0.12"/>0<ce:hsp sp="0.12"/>0) and Cu(1<ce:hsp sp="0.12"/>1<ce:hsp sp="0.12"/>1) substrates, and it demonstrates outstanding stability in air and organic solvents. These characteristics show its potential as a promising anode material for metal-ion batteries.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0050" view="all">Motivated by the remarkable stability and perfect planar checkerboard lattice structure of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, in this study, we investigate its potential as an anode material for LIBS and SIBs. We begin our investigation with a thorough examination of the adsorption behavior of Li/Na, with a particular emphasis on adsorption energies, binding distance, charge transfer, and charge density differences. The monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N shows great promise as an anode material in terms of its ultralow diffusion barrier, high electrical conductivity owing to its metallic nature, minimal volume expansion, and appreciable adsorption energies. We also examine the effect of Cu and N vacancy defects on the electrochemical properties of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, as vacancy defects are almost inevitable during material synthesis. Our study demonstrated that vacancy defects in monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N significantly enhance Li/Na binding and also lower the Li diffusion barrier in the case of Cu vacancies. These results highlight the promise of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N as anodes for battery applications.</ce:para>
               </ce:section>
               <ce:section id="s0010" view="all">
                  <ce:label>2</ce:label>
                  <ce:section-title id="st035">Computational details</ce:section-title>
                  <ce:para id="p0055" view="all">The Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP)<ce:cross-refs refid="b0195 b0200" id="r0065">[39,40]</ce:cross-refs> was used to perform all the density-functional theory calculations by employing the projector-augmented wave method. The exchange–correlation functional was modeled using the generalized gradient approximation (GGA)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0205" id="c0060">[41]</ce:cross-ref> scheme developed by Perdew, Burke, and Ernzerhof (PBE). The empirical correction approach suggested by Grimme (DFT + D3)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0210" id="c0065">[42]</ce:cross-ref> was used to implement the van der Waals interactions. The kinetic energy cutoff of 500 eV was set for the plane wave basis expansion. A 15 × 15 × 1 and 3 × 3 × 1 Monkhorst-Pack k-grid<ce:cross-ref refid="b0215" id="c0070">[43]</ce:cross-ref> was adapted for the Brillouin zone integration of the unit cell and the 3 × 3 × 1 supercell of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. The energy and force convergence thresholds were established at 10<ce:sup loc="post">−6</ce:sup> eV and 0.05 eV/Å, respectively. To prevent spurious interactions caused by periodic images, a 20 Å vacuum was added to the <ce:italic>z</ce:italic>-axis. The Bader charge<ce:cross-ref refid="b0220" id="c0075">[44]</ce:cross-ref> method was used to calculate the amount of charge transfer between monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N and adsorbates (Li/Na). The adsorption energies (<ce:italic>E<ce:inf loc="post">ad</ce:inf>
                     </ce:italic>) for the adsorbates on Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N surface are determined according to the following equation:<ce:display>

The climbing-image nudged elastic band method (CI-NEB)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0225" id="c0080">[45]</ce:cross-ref> was used to compute the diffusion barriers and minimum energy paths (MEP) for adsorbate atoms on the Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N surface. NEB calculations were performed on seven intermediate images between the initial and final states, which were then verified by optimizing the structures until the tangential forces on each image reached values less than 0.01 eV/Å.</ce:para>
               </ce:section>
               <ce:section id="s0015" view="all">
                  <ce:label>3</ce:label>
                  <ce:section-title id="st040">Results and discussions</ce:section-title>
                  <ce:para id="p0060" view="all">The monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N crystallizes in a tetragonal configuration with space group P4/mmm (No. 123) as shown in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0005" id="c0085">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="f0005"/> (a) and (b). The primitive unit cell of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N contains two Cu and one N atom, which occupy the 2f and 1a Wyckoff positions, respectively. The optimized lattice parameters of ML Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N are <ce:italic>a</ce:italic> = <ce:italic>b</ce:italic> = 3.65 Å, with cell angles α = <ce:italic>β</ce:italic> = <ce:italic>γ</ce:italic> = 90°. Each Cu atom forms covalent bonds with two adjacent N atoms and four Cu atoms with a bond length of 1.83 Å and 2.58 Å, respectively. The corresponding BZ with high-symmetry points is shown in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0005" id="c0090">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref>c. The electronic band structure, along with the projected density of states (PDOS) are depicted in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0005" id="c0095">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref>(d). It is clear that the system is highly metallic, which suggests its exceptional electronic conductivity, rendering it a promising choice for a metal-ion battery anode. Further, the PDOS shows that the low-energy states near the Fermi level are mainly composed of Cu <ce:italic>d</ce:italic> orbitals. These findings are consistent with those reported by Hu <ce:italic>et al</ce:italic>
                     <ce:cross-ref refid="b0190" id="c0100">[38]</ce:cross-ref>.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0065" view="all">To examine the adsorption of a single Li/Na atom on monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, we constructed a 3 × 3 × 1 supercell with possible adsorption sites I, II, and III as shown in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0005" id="c9412">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref> (a). Initially, the Li/Na atom is positioned 3.00 Å above the sites I, II, and III to find the preferred adsorption site . Subsequently, these configurations are relaxed until the criteria for geometry optimization are satisfied. According to the computed adsorption energies (<ce:cross-ref refid="f0010" id="c0110">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="f0010"/> (a)), the lowest energy state for a single Li atom is at site II, with subsequent favorable sites being I and III. In contrast, the optimal binding site for a single Na atom is site I, followed by sites III and II. The strongest adsorption energy for Li is −1.80 eV, and for Na, it is −1.70 eV, which are deemed sufficient to avoid the formation of metallic dendrites and achieve the desired voltage<ce:cross-ref refid="b0230" id="c0115">[46]</ce:cross-ref>. The corresponding DOS for single Li and Na adsorb monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N are shown in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0010" id="c0120">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref>(b)−(c), respectively. Clearly, the metallic characteristics of the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N persist after the adsorption of metal-ions, which is beneficial for its potential use as anode material.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0070" view="all">To determine the bonding nature between adsorbing Li/Na-ion and Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, the electron localization function (ELF) is analyzed. The ELF values vary from 0 to 1, with 0 denoting electron delocalization, 0.5 denoting an electron–gas state, and 1 denoting complete electron localization. The ELF plots, shown in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0010" id="c0125">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref>d, reveal the formation of an electron gas-like state on the surface of the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. Neither Li nor Na displays any localized electron basins in binding with the surface that are connected with chemical bonds in ELF. Instead, there is strong repulsion between the metal atoms and the surface, with bands of zero ELF between the interacting atoms, which is typical for ionic binding. The formation of strong but reversible ionic binding between Li/Na and Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N<ce:cross-ref refid="b0235" id="c0130">[47]</ce:cross-ref> is beneficial in avoiding Li/Na clustering and favorable diffusion rates of Li/Na on the Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N surface. Furthermore, <ce:cross-ref refid="f0010" id="c0135">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref> (e) and (f) depict the charge density differences and amount of charge transfer between metal atoms and monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. Clearly, the charge is depleted from the Li/Na atom and accumulates between Li/Na and the Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N sheet, as indicated by the cyan and yellow clouds in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0010" id="c0140">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref>(e) and (f). To confirm these findings, Bader charge analysis is conducted, which verifies a charge transfer of 0.91/0.49 e from Li/Na to the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0075" view="all">Next, a stepwise adsorption of metal ions is carried out to anticipate the maximum storage capacity of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. To do that, we set two criteria: First, ensure that the Li–Li/Na–Na distance is larger than the bond length in Li<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>(2.67 Å)/Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>(3.08 Å)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0240" id="c0145">[48]</ce:cross-ref> in order to prevent metal clustering. Second, verify that the chemical potential of the Li and Na on the Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N anode is less than or equal to that of the Li/Na atom (μ<ce:inf loc="post">Li/Na</ce:inf>)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0245" id="c0150">[49]</ce:cross-ref>. This needs<ce:display>

If both <ce:italic>PV</ce:italic> and <ce:italic>TS</ce:italic> terms are ignored, the above equation can be expressed as follows:<ce:display>

                     </ce:display>By combining Eq. <ce:cross-ref refid="e0025" id="c0155">(5)</ce:cross-ref> and Eq. <ce:cross-ref refid="e0030" id="c0160">(6)</ce:cross-ref>, we get:<ce:display>

                           <ce:label>(7)</ce:label>

                              <mml:mrow>
                                 <mml:mfrac>
                                    <mml:mrow>
                                       <mml:mi>∂</mml:mi>
                                       <mml:msub>
                                          <mml:mi>E</mml:mi>
                                          <mml:mi>f</mml:mi>

                                 </mml:mfrac>

                                 <mml:mn>0</mml:mn>
                              </mml:mrow>
                           </mml:math>
                        </ce:formula>
                     </ce:display>This demonstrates that a negative slope of the formation energy curve is necessary for the Li/Na loading process. Therefore, we continue to add one more Li/Na to all possible free positions to find the preferred adsorption site for it and take the configuration with the lowest energy and continue adding Li/Na until either all possible positions are filled or the <ce:italic>E<ce:inf loc="post">f</ce:inf>
                     </ce:italic> slope becomes positive. The Li/Na atoms are first loaded on the top side of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, then on the bottom side. Finally, the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N can accommodate a total of 18 Li/Na atoms, with nine at the top and nine at the bottom. Importantly, the <ce:italic>E<ce:inf loc="post">f</ce:inf>
                     </ce:italic> slope remains negative, showing the possibility for more Li/Na storage (see <ce:cross-ref refid="f0015" id="c0165">Fig. 3</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="f0015"/>(a) and (b)). Further, the shortest Li–Li/Na–Na distance on fully loaded monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N is around 3.65 Å, which is sufficiently large to avoid Li/Na atom clustering. The storage capacity of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N for both Li and Na is 379.88 mAh.g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>, exceeding the predicted storage capacity of graphite (372 mAh.g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> for Li and &lt; 35 mAh.g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> for Na)<ce:cross-refs refid="b0250 b0255" id="r0075">[50,51]</ce:cross-refs>.</ce:para>

                     </mml:math> and <ce:italic>x<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>
                     </ce:italic> and <ce:italic>x<ce:inf loc="post">1</ce:inf>
                     </ce:italic> represent the two adjacent concentrations of adsorbed Li/Na ions on Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. Four voltage plateaus are obtained for both Li and Na, with an average OCV of about 1.39 V and 0.82 V, respectively.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0090" view="all">Now, we look at the carrier mobility and diffusivity of metal ions on the Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N monolayer. The CI-NEB method is employed to calculate the diffusion path of metal ions and their corresponding minimal energy profile (MEP) for a single Li/Na atom scattered on the surface of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. The top views of the scattering paths evaluated in this work, together with their corresponding energy profiles, are depicted in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0020" id="c0175">Fig. 4</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="f0020"/>. For Li, Path 1 implies the diffusion of a Li atom from the most stable site (II), characterized by the highest <ce:italic>E<ce:inf loc="post">ad</ce:inf>
                     </ce:italic>, to the nearest (II) site in the <ce:italic>ab</ce:italic>-direction. On the other hand, in Path 2, the Li atom diffuses over the (I) site to reach the nearest stable (II) site, following the <ce:italic>a</ce:italic>-direction. Clearly, the Li atom prefers to diffuse via Path 2, with a calculated diffusion barrier of 0.1 eV, while the diffusion barrier for Path 1 is 0.19 eV. Similarly, in the case of Na, Path 1 implies the diffusion of a Na atom from the most stable site (I) to the nearest (I) site along the <ce:italic>ab</ce:italic>-direction, while Path 2 involves the Na atom diffusing to the nearest stable site (I) along the <ce:italic>a</ce:italic>-direction. The Na atom prefers diffusing through Path 1, with a calculated diffusion barrier of 0.01 eV, while the diffusion barrier for Path 2 is 0.053 eV. The variation in diffusion barriers along different directions is not available in many well-known 2D materials including graphene, MoS<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>, VS<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>, and Ti<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>V<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>
                     <ce:cross-refs refid="b0150 b0260 b0265 b0270 b0275" id="r0080">[30,52–55]</ce:cross-refs>. This uniaxial anisotropic movement of Li/Na significantly reduces the scattering effect, which is highly advantageous for LIBs/SIBs. Besides, our predicted Li barrier for Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N is significantly lower than that of graphene (0.32 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0280" id="c0180">[56]</ce:cross-ref> and other prominent commercial Li-anodes like TiO<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> (0.4–1.0 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0285" id="c0185">[57]</ce:cross-ref> and silicon (0.6–0.8 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0290" id="c0190">[58]</ce:cross-ref>. It is even less than the barriers predicted in several 2D materials such as BC<ce:inf loc="post">7</ce:inf> (0.67 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0295" id="c0195">[59]</ce:cross-ref>, MoS<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> (0.17 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0275" id="c0200">[55]</ce:cross-ref>, Ta<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>CS<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> (0.21 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0300" id="c0205">[60]</ce:cross-ref>, holey graphyne (0.28 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0105" id="c0210">[21]</ce:cross-ref>, graphdiyne (0.7 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0305" id="c0215">[61]</ce:cross-ref>, C<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>N/blue phosphorene (0.12 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0310" id="c0220">[62]</ce:cross-ref>, VS<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>/blue phosphorene (0.16–0.17 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0315" id="c0225">[63]</ce:cross-ref>, MoSSe (0.17 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0320" id="c0230">[64]</ce:cross-ref>, and VS<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> (0.22 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0265" id="c0235">[53]</ce:cross-ref>, while being comparable with Ti<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>C<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> (0.068 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0270" id="c0240">[54]</ce:cross-ref>, V<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>C (0.045 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0155" id="c0245">[31]</ce:cross-ref>, aluminene (0.06 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0060" id="c0250">[12]</ce:cross-ref>, and phosphorene (0.08 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0065" id="c0255">[13]</ce:cross-ref>. As for Na, its diffusion barrier is smaller than that of TiC (0.1 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0185" id="c0260">[37]</ce:cross-ref>, borophene (0.33 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0070" id="c0265">[14]</ce:cross-ref>, Ca<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N (0.16 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0075" id="c0270">[15]</ce:cross-ref>, h-BAs (0.25 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0325" id="c0275">[65]</ce:cross-ref>, β-Sb (0.1 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0080" id="c0280">[16]</ce:cross-ref>, borophane (0.09 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0085" id="c0285">[17]</ce:cross-ref>, Ti<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>C<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> (0.1 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0270" id="c0290">[54]</ce:cross-ref>, NbS<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> (0.07)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0020" id="c0295">[4]</ce:cross-ref>, and comparable with Nb<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>C (0.02)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0330" id="c0300">[66]</ce:cross-ref>, V<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N (0.01)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0335" id="c0305">[67]</ce:cross-ref>, and aluminene (0.03)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0060" id="c0310">[12]</ce:cross-ref>. This suggests that monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N possesses high ionic mobility and a rapid charge/discharge, both of which are desirable for an excellent anode material.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0095" view="all">The structure distortion of anodes during the charging and discharging processes is one of the major causes of damage to metal-ion batteries. Recent studies have demonstrated that anode materials with self-healing ability can significantly improve the cycle life of metal-ion batteries. To evaluate the cyclic stability of the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, we remove the metal ions and perform the <ce:italic>ai</ce:italic>MD simulation on the remaining Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N sheet at 300 K. In <ce:cross-ref refid="f0025" id="c0315">Fig. 5</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="f0025"/>, the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N swiftly restored its initial state within a mere 2.5 ps, showing its favorable cyclic performance. Further, to identify the volume expansion of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N during the lithiation and sodiation processes, a bilayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N structure with Li/Na atoms between the layers is used to estimate the distance change of the Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N bilayers after Li/Na intercalation, as shown in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0030" id="c0320">Fig. 6</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="f0030"/>. Consequently, Li-intercalation causes a volume expansion of 4.14 % (4.02 Å after lithiation from 3.86 Å for pristine Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N bilayer), which is significantly less than the 10 % expansion predicted for graphite<ce:cross-ref refid="b0340" id="c0325">[68]</ce:cross-ref>. As for Na, it exhibits a volume expansion of 35.23 % (5.22 Å) owing to its larger size. However, this value is far smaller than the estimated 347 % expansion of Na intercalated graphite along the <ce:italic>c</ce:italic>-axis<ce:cross-ref refid="b0345" id="c0330">[69]</ce:cross-ref>.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0100" view="all">Vacancy creation during electrode manufacture or with lattice vibrations at high temperatures is always a possibility<ce:cross-ref refid="b0185" id="c0335">[37]</ce:cross-ref>. Therefore, examining the effect of vacancy formations on the electronic conductivity of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N is desirable. In <ce:cross-ref refid="f0035" id="c0340">Fig. 7</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="f0035"/>, we introduced single Cu and N-vacancy defects on the 3 × 3 × 1 supercell of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. The formation energies for Cu and N vacancies are 5.43 eV and 8.97 eV, respectively. This demonstrates that Cu vacancies occur more readily than N vacancies in monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. The computed vacancy formation energy values are comparable to those of graphene (7.7 eV)<ce:cross-ref refid="b0350" id="c0345">[70]</ce:cross-ref>, suggesting a strong bonding between Cu–Cu and Cu–N in Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. Moreover, the N vacancy exhibits a nonmagnetic ground state, which is consistent with pristine Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. Conversely, the Cu vacancy results in a magnetic moment of 2.07 µB in the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. The vacancy-induced magnetism has also been observed in graphene.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0105" view="all">Next, we examine the adsorption of Li-ion and Na-ion on the vacancy-defected 3 × 3 × 1 supercell of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N monolayer with four possible sites, as shown in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0035" id="c0350">Fig. 7</ce:cross-ref> (a) and (b). As shown in <ce:cross-ref refid="t0005" id="c0355">Table 1</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="t0005"/>, in the case of Cu vacancy, both Li and Na preferred to adsorb at the top of the vacancy site Cu<ce:inf loc="post">Cu</ce:inf> (<ce:italic>E<ce:inf loc="post">ad</ce:inf>
                     </ce:italic> = -3.33 eV for Li and −3.34 eV for Na). For N vacancy as well, Cu site V<ce:inf loc="post">Cu</ce:inf> (<ce:italic>E<ce:inf loc="post">ad</ce:inf>
                     </ce:italic> = -1.90 eV for Li and −1.44 eV for Na) is energetically more favorable. In contrast to graphene, here, the introduction of vacancy defects substantially enhances the Li/Na binding ability, indicating the positive effect of vacancy defects on the surface. To estimate the charging–discharging rate of vacancy-defected Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, we calculate Li and Na diffusion barriers on the surface. In the Cu vacancy case, where Li/Na adsorption is favorable at the vacancy site, we calculate the diffusion of Li/Na between two adjacent equivalent sites with a Cu vacancy site positioned in the middle (see <ce:cross-ref refid="f0040" id="c0360">Fig. 8</ce:cross-ref>
                     <ce:float-anchor refid="f0040"/> (a) and (b)). The calculated migration barriers for Li and Na are 0.07 and 0.096 eV, respectively. Notably, the migration barrier for Li is relatively low compared to pristine Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N, whereas the migration barrier for Na is comparable to pristine Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N. Similarly, in the N vacancy case, we consider the migration of Li/Na atoms from the stable site V<ce:inf loc="post">Cu</ce:inf> to the nearest V<ce:inf loc="post">Cu</ce:inf> site while passing over the N vacancy, as illustrated in <ce:cross-ref refid="f0040" id="c0365">Fig. 8</ce:cross-ref> (c) and (d). The corresponding migration barriers for Li and Na are 0.23 and 0.09 eV, respectively. These values are a bit higher than in pure Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N but still very low when compared with other 2D anode materials, demonstrating their suitability for metal-ion battery applications.</ce:para>
               </ce:section>
               <ce:section id="s0020" view="all">
                  <ce:label>4</ce:label>
                  <ce:section-title id="st045">Conclusion</ce:section-title>
                  <ce:para id="p0110" view="all">We have investigated the potential of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N as an anode material for Li and Na-ion batteries using first-principles calculations. Our calculations show that the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N has a strong affinity for Li and Na atoms, with negative adsorption energies of around 1.80 and 1.43 eV, respectively. The calculated storage capacity for both Li and Na is determined to be 379.88 mAh.g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>. Furthermore, the monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N exhibits ultra-low migration barriers of 0.10 eV for Li and 0.01 eV for Na, significantly surpassing the migration barriers found in commercial graphite and most of the known 2D materials. The insertion of Li and Na atoms into the interlayer space of Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N induces a slight swelling effect that results in volume expansion of 4.14 and 35.23 %, respectively. Furthermore, the presence of vacancy defects enhanced the binding energies and decreased the Li diffusion barrier. This suggests that, in contrast to commercial anode materials such as graphene, vacancy defects are not hazardous to monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N for use in metal-ion batteries. These findings provide valuable insights for the experimental implementation of monolayer Cu<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>N as a potential anode material for metal-ion batteries.</ce:para>
               </ce:section>
               <ce:section id="s0025" view="all">
